Executive Summary

The  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA),  under  the  Implementation  Monitoring  and
Evaluation Division (IMED) of the Ministry of Planning, serves as the principal regulatory body for
public procurement governance in Bangladesh. In pursuit of its mandate to strengthen public financial
management, BPPA conducts Procurement Post-Reviews (PPR) under Section 24 of PPA-2006 and
Rule  47  of  PPR-2008.  The  Department  of  Agricultural  Extension  (DAE),  under  the  Ministry  of
Agriculture, plays a pivotal role in driving sustainable agricultural growth. This review, commissioned
by  BPPA  and  conducted  by  an  independent  consultant,  evaluates  procurement  performance,
compliance, and operational effectiveness across five major projects implemented by DAE during the
2023–2024 fiscal year.
The  review  examined  30  procurement  contracts  across  goods,  works,  and  consultancy  services,
applying  robust  fieldwork,  documentary  analysis,  site  inspections,  and  stakeholder  consultations.  It
aimed to validate compliance with legal frameworks, assess value for money, and identify procedural
strengths  and  weaknesses.  Comprehensive  on-site  observations  across  several  districts  ensured  that
practical realities of contract implementation were captured.
Findings suggest that the Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE) has generally adopted sound
procurement  practices,  with  many  contracts  showing  reasonable  compliance  and  cost  efficiency—
actual contract prices in most cases were observed to be below official estimates. Nonetheless, certain
areas require attention, particularly the prolonged tender evaluation periods for works contracts and
limited  supplier  participation.  Expanding  outreach  to  regional  suppliers  could  help  improve  market
responsiveness and competition.
A Value for Money (VfM) assessment found that public resources were generally used with due regard
to economy, efficiency, effectiveness, and equity. Conducted at the project level, the assessment drew
on  field  visits  and  end-user  interviews  to  evaluate  on-ground  outcomes.  Competitive  procurement
methods  and  compliance  with  the  PPA  2006  and  PPR  2008  underpinned  credible  processes.
Investments  in  ICT,  agricultural  equipment,  laboratories,  and  training  supported  measurable
improvements in productivity, resilience, and service delivery. Gender and regional inclusion further
reflected DAE’s commitment to equitable and sustainable development.
Nonetheless, non-compliance issues were identified in the management of performance securities and
insurance documentation. Several contracts under the EIVTSP project did not meet mandated security
thresholds  or  warranty  coverage  requirements.  The  absence  of  insurance  documentation  in  work
contracts  also  raised  concerns  about  risk  mitigation.  Contract management  practices  showed  mixed
results, with documentation gaps regarding time extensions and supplier performance monitoring.
The  review  recommends  targeted  capacity-building  initiatives,  including  strengthening  post-
qualification  diligence,  improving  record-keeping,  reinforcing  performance-based  oversight,  and
promoting internationally recognized  certifications  such  as  MCIPS  among  DAE  officials.  Adopting
best  practices—such  as  centralized  digital  contract  repositories  and  broader  tender  dissemination
strategies—will further enhance transparency and efficiency.
In  conclusion,  the  Procurement  Post-Review  highlights  DAE’s  continued  efforts  to  strengthen
procurement accountability and improve operational practices. By progressively adopting appropriate
learned,  and
remedial  measures—such  as  procurement-focused

training,  applying

lessons

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(ix)

implementing targeted improvements—DAE, along with the wider public procurement system, is well
positioned  to  advance  service  delivery  and  promote  more  effective  use  of  public  resources  across
Bangladesh.

Lt Commander (retd) Syed Abdus Samad, MCIPS
Individual Consultant

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(x)

Chapter 1

Introduction

1.1 Introduction
The  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA),  under  the  Implementation  Monitoring  and
Evaluation Division (IMED) of the Ministry of Planning, is the principal regulatory body for public
procurement governance in Bangladesh. Reconstituted from the former Central Procurement Technical
Unit (CPTU) under the Bangladesh Public Procurement Authority Act, 2023 (Act No. 32 of 2023),
BPPA is mandated to develop procurement policies, build institutional capacity, operate the national e-
Government  Procurement  (e-GP)  system,  and  monitor  compliance.  It  functions  under  a  Governing
Board  chaired  by  the  Honourable  Minister  of  Planning,  with  the  Chief  Executive  Officer  (CEO)
overseeing daily operations. BPPA’s activities are central to promoting transparency, accountability,
good governance, and efficient public service delivery.

Public procurement serves as a vital indicator of a nation’s economic activity, reflecting the efficiency
and accountability of public expenditure. Ensuring transparency, effectiveness, and value for money in
public procurement has remained a priority for governments globally, and Bangladesh is no exception.
To strengthen its public procurement framework, Bangladesh embarked on reform initiatives in 2002,
implementing legal and institutional frameworks alongside capacity-building programs. Over the past
22 years, significant strides have been made in institutionalizing a robust public procurement system,
grounded  in  the  legal  provisions  of  the  Public  Procurement  Act  2006  (PPA-2006)  and  the  Public
Procurement Rules 2008 (PPR-2008).

The Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE), operating under the Ministry of Agriculture, stands
as the largest provider of agricultural and technology extension services in Bangladesh. Established in
1960, the DAE has played a pivotal role in advancing agricultural practices. Following the country's
independence, the Government of the People's Republic of Bangladesh intensified efforts to bolster
agricultural extension initiatives, resulting in the establishment of specialized bodies such as the Cotton
Development Board, the Tobacco Development Board, and the Horticulture Board.

1.2 Background and Context
As  a  part  of  Institutional  Reform,  the  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA)  formerly
known as Central Procurement Technical Unit (CPTU) in IMED, Ministry of Planning was established
and it is mandated to implement the reform to ensure transparency, efficiency, and accountability to
acquire value for money in public expenditure.

PPA-2006; Section 24 clearly states the clause Post Procurement Review as follows:

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(1)

(1)  The Procuring Entity shall, within nine months of the end of each fiscal year, arrange for
an impartial post-procurement review, on a sample basis, of its total procuring activities
during the preceding year.

(2)  The authority empowered by the government on this behalf may review post-procurement

activities carried out by a procuring entity.

(3)  The review under sub-sections (1) & (2) shall follow the random sample method and the

outline of such method may be prescribed by rules.

As per Section-24 of the PPA-2006 and Rules-45 & 46 of the PPR-2008, BPPA was entrusted with the
responsibility to Review and Monitor the progress of implementation of the PPA-2006 and PPR-2008
in  procurement  management  in  government  agencies.  According  to  the  Rules,  the  records  of
procurement activities of the Procuring Entities (PE) shall be reviewed by BPPA using its resources by
hiring Independent Consultants. The criteria for selecting any Procuring Entity would be the value of
procurement performed in a financial year worth at least Taka 10.00 crore. The Procuring Entity to be
reviewed will be specified by the BPPA and the Independent Consultants will verify the compliance of
the provisions of the Act and Rules taken in the procurement process.

1.3 Objectives of the Assignment
The primary objective of this assignment, as outlined in the Terms of Reference (ToR), is to promote
the efficient, effective, and economical utilization of resources in public procurement. This involves
assessing compliance with the provisions of the Public Procurement Act, 2006 (PPA, 2006), the Public
Procurement Rules, 2008 (PPR, 2008), and the guidelines or regulations of development partners. By
meticulously  reviewing  records  and  relevant  documentation  associated  with  sample  contracts,  the
assignment aims to ensure adherence to these standards while safeguarding value for money in public
procurement activities.

As per the communicated ToR, the specific objectives are to:

1.

2.

determine whether the procurement processes and contracting proceedings are in accordance
with the Public Procurement Acts and Rules;
 review whether the official cost estimate has been prepared as per PPR, 2008 and whether it
was modified by the approving authority and opened as per rules;

3.  review whether STDs are followed & tender documents are prepared in line with the acts and

rules;

4.  assess the extent to which Value-for-Money is being achieved in the procurement process as

required by the Procuring Entities;

5.  Review and access the capacity of the concerned Procuring Entities in handling procurement
from  document  preparation  to  contract  implementation  and  document  management  as  per
Rules & required standards;

6.  review and assess that the procurements made are being used for the intended purpose;
7.

identify  whether  improvement  achieved  in  procurement  activities  in  light  of  deficiencies
identified in the earlier reviewer.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(2)

1.4 Scope of the Assignment
The scope of this assignment encompasses a thorough review of the procurement processes carried out
by Procuring Entities that have executed procurement activities amounting to at least Taka 10.00 crore
in a financial year, or as stipulated under Rule 46(7) of the Public Procurement Rules (PPR), 2008, on
a sample basis. Specifically, the Individual Consultant will evaluate procurement contracts completed
or  awarded  during  the  financial  year  2023-2024.  This  review  will involve  an  assessment  of  six  (6)
procurement  contracts  from  each  of  five  (5)  selected  procurement  entities  of  the  Government  of
Bangladesh.  The  Consultant's  primary  focus  was  to  conduct  a  procurement  post-review  for  the
Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE).  The consultant did review the following:

a.  Verify  the  procurement  and  contracting  proceeding  executed  by  the  procuring  entity  and

identify non-compliance with the procurement Act and Rules;

b.  verify whether PE has used Standard Tender Documents (STDs) and Standard Requests for
Proposals (SRfPs) in its Procurement content & put the necessary information in Tender Data
Sheet (TDS), Proposal data Sheet (PDS) and other formats correctly.

c.  verify,  to  the  extent  possible,  whether  the  Goods,  Works  and  Services  contracted  were
supplied/performed  and/or  completed  according  to  the  required  specifications and  technical
standards;
identify inappropriate practices or questionable decisions and/or actions;

d.
e.  verify whether documentation and record-keeping systems that are in place are adequate for

ensuring post-review requirements;

f.  verify whether goods and works exist at intended locations and are being used for the purposes

they were acquired;

g.  determine remedies to correct the identified deficiencies.

For each contract package, the consultant reviewed the following key elements (Annex-1-2):

a.  Procurement plan, budget, official cost estimate;
b.  Procurement  type,  category,  method,  objective,  financing  arrangement  (in  case  of  DP’s

supported project);

c.  Procurement opportunities (advertisements in the newspaper and on the website);
d.  Tender/proposal  submissions  (no  of  sale/issuance  of  tender/proposal  documents,  no  of

participants, days allows for preparation and submission);

e.  Formation of TOC/POC and TEC/PEC (no of members, external members);
f.  Tenders/Proposals Evaluation (opening, days between opening and evaluation, responsiveness

(responsive & non-responsive), recommendations of the evaluation committee);

g.  Post qualification;
h.  Approval  of  tenders/proposals  (actual  days  between  opening  &  evaluation  and  between
evaluation & approval, approval authority as delegation of financial power, actual approval
authority, actual days for issuance of NOA/contract signing/LOI);

i.  Contract award (procurement processing lag & lead time);
j.  Completion  of  contract  (time  specified  for  supply/execution/perform/Pre-shipment/Post

Landing Inspection);

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(3)

k.  Complaints  and  appeal  (reason  of  complain,  resolution  of  complaints,  decision  of  review

panel);

l.  Events of realising liquated damage (if any);
m.  Contract  amendment  (no  of  amendment,  contract  time  extension,  variation/extra  work/

repeat/additional delivery orders etc.);

n.  Contract disputes unresolved;
o.  Action on corruption, fraudulence, collusive and coercive if identified;
p.  Procurement management capacity (no of staff trained in procurement) etc.
q.  Procurement Plan/Annual Procurement plan, Budget, Official cost estimate.
r.  Audit objection and status.

All relevant data be collected and documentary evidence of the data should be submitted with a report
to BPPA

1.5 Analysis and Review Framework

1.5.1 Significance of Procurement Post-Review
Procurement post-review serves as a cornerstone of procurement management, offering a structured
approach to identify lessons learned, highlight best practices, and foster continuous improvement in
future procurement activities. This review aims to delve into the multifaceted aspects of procurement
post-review, encompassing its significance, methodologies, challenges, and benefits, with a specific
focus on its application in selected contracts.

As  a  reflective  process,  procurement  post-review  enables  organizations  to  evaluate  successes  and
shortcomings, offering critical insights into what worked effectively and what did not. By implementing
these learnings, organizations can enhance their procurement practices and ensure improved outcomes.
Additionally, it facilitates the assessment of supplier performance, adherence to contractual obligations,
and the overall efficacy of procurement processes, underscoring its indispensable role in organizational
growth and accountability.

1.5.2 Framework for Data Analysis
The  procurement  post-review  process  is  integral  to  evaluating  the  efficiency  and  effectiveness  of
procurement  practices  within  an  organization  or  procuring  entities.  During  the  review  of  various
Procuring  Entities  (PEs)  within  the  Department  of  Agricultural  Extension  (DAE),  diverse
methodologies and procedures were adopted to collect data on performance metrics, compliance with
the Public Procurement Act, and adherence to relevant rules and guidelines.

The assignment commenced with a kick-off meeting involving the Head of Procuring Entity (HOPE),
the Director General (DG) of DAE, and senior officials. The DG exhibited strong support, instructing
the  concerned  PEs  to  provide  all  necessary  assistance  for  the  post-review  activities.  To  streamline
communication,  the  DG  also  appointed  a  Focal  Person  to  liaise  between  the  DAE,  the  Bangladesh
Public Procurement Authority (BPPA), and the consultant.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(4)

Initial interviews were conducted with five Project Directors and officers responsible for procurement
activities. In consultation with these Project Directors, procurement samples were finalized for review.
This process involved selecting 99 contracts as the initial sample. To facilitate the review, each Project
Director designated a Monitoring Officer to provide data and documentation support. Over a 15-day
period,  the  procurement  samples  were  finalized  through  collaborative  discussions  with  the  Project
Directors.
Following further deliberation with the BPPA and relevant officials, the sample size was refined to 30
contracts.  With  the  selection  finalized,  the  preliminary  phase  of  the  procurement  post-review  was
successfully completed, enabling a seamless transition into the second phase.

1.5.3 Evaluation of Procurement Documents
The  analysis  of  procurement  documents  plays  a  vital  role  in  identifying  trends,  discrepancies,  and
opportunities for improvement in procurement practices. Key aspects include reviewing the preparation
and approval of official cost estimates, the advertisement process for wide circulation, issuance of EOIs,
RFPs, and RFQs, opening and evaluation of tenders, issuance of NOAs, performance security validity
and extensions, liquidated damages, contract terms, adherence to STDs, contractor invoices, timeliness
of  payments,  and  contract  dispute  resolution  mechanisms.  This  comprehensive  approach  reveals
compliance levels with PPA-2006 and PPR-2008 while highlighting areas for refinement.

The procurement document analysis involved observational studies, benchmarking, data analytics, and
documentary reviews to ensure a comprehensive evaluation.

➢  Observational studies provided firsthand insights into procurement activities, assessing policy

adherence, contractor relationships, and operational inefficiencies.

➢  Benchmarking  measured  procurement  efficiency  by  comparing  cycle  times  to  industry
standards, analyzing cost variances, evaluating bid evaluation timelines, and assessing supplier
performance and dispute resolution effectiveness.

➢  Data  analytics  examined  competition  levels,  procurement  planning  efficiency,  spending
trends,  supplier  compliance,  and  contract  performance.  Analytical  tools  identified  trends  in
disputes, payment delays, and adherence to performance standards.

➢  Documentary  reviews  involved  analyzing  procurement  records,  including  advertisements,
EOIs,  RFPs,  RFQs,  and  evaluation  reports.  The  review  also  verified  NOAs,  performance
securities, contract amendments, liquidated damage claims, and payment timelines.

This structured approach provided a holistic assessment of procurement efficiency, compliance, and
overall performance.

1.5.4 Review of Literature and Documentation

The  effectiveness  of  the  procurement  post-review  process  is  reinforced  through  comprehensive
literature  reviews,  analysis  of  the  Terms  of  Reference  (ToR),  and  insights  gathered  from  BPPA
meetings, including kick-off, progress, and technical committee discussions.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(5)

A critical component of the review involved analyzing the Development Project Proforma (DPP) to
assess project objectives, cost estimates, timelines, funding arrangements, and procurement strategies,
ensuring  alignment  with  overall  project  goals  while  identifying  potential  risks.  The  procurement
contracts were examined for adherence to terms and conditions, including pricing, delivery schedules,
and quality benchmarks, to verify value for money and risk mitigation. Additionally, change orders and
amendments were reviewed for compliance with procurement regulations, proper authorization, and
their impact on project execution.

The  review  also  covered  bid  evaluation  reports  to  assess  the  transparency  and  efficiency  of  tender
evaluations,  ensuring  compliance  with  procurement  regulations.  Additional  supply  orders,  variation
orders, and repeat orders were evaluated to determine necessity, regulatory adherence, and potential
risks. Delivery receipts, reports, and completion certificates were scrutinized to confirm compliance
with contract specifications, delays, damages, or incomplete work.

A thorough examination of invoices and payment records was conducted to verify timely payments
under  PPR  2008,  identifying  discrepancies  or  unresolved  payments.  The  procurement  plan  and
schedules were cross-checked against actual procurement activities to  assess adherence to approved
timelines  and  milestones.  The  review  also  included  an  evaluation  of  performance  security  and
guarantees to ensure validity, compliance, and proper handling of liquidated damages in cases of non-
compliance or delays.

Further  assessment  covered  dispute  resolution  mechanisms,  reviewing  arbitration  records  and  other
settlement  processes  to  gauge  their  effectiveness  and  fairness.  Supplier  and  contractor  performance
reports  were  analyzed  to  determine  compliance  with  contractual  obligations,  including  the  quality,
timeliness,  and  reliability  of  delivered  goods,  works,  and  services.  Lastly,  the  review  examined
environmental  and  social  compliance  to  ensure  procurement  activities  aligned  with  sustainability
guidelines, verifying the implementation of necessary mitigation measures.

1.5.5 On-Site Inspections and Observations

Field Visits and Monitoring Activities: The core activities of DAE projects include fostering socio-
economic  development  among  farmers,  introducing  new  crop  varieties,  procuring  seeds  and
agricultural machinery,  and  delivering hands-on  training to  farmers.  Given  the nationwide  scope  of
these initiatives, data was collected through selective field visits to project areas in various upzillas of
Sylhet, Habiganj, Sunamganj and Moulvibazar districts, horticulture centers of Sirajganj and Pabna,
BSMRAU in Salna, Gazipur, and Bangladesh Agricultural University of Mymensingh. These on-site
inspections provided valuable insights into the practical implementation of project activities and the
challenges faced at the grassroots level.

1.5.6 Stakeholder and Beneficiary Engagement:

Interviews with Key Stakeholders: Interviews were conducted with key stakeholders involved in the
procurement process, offering critical insights into project activities, successes, and challenges. During
Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(6)

the  field  visits,  additional  sporadic  interviews  were  held  with  beneficiaries  and  other  stakeholders.
These discussions yielded valuable qualitative information about project implementation, procurement
practices, and the impact of interventions on target groups.
1.5.7 Key Findings and Insights:

The use of multiple methodologies enabled a thorough analysis of procurement practices, identifying
key  strengths,  weaknesses,  and  areas  for  improvement.  Each  approach  contributed  unique  insights,
fostering  a  comprehensive  understanding  of  procurement  performance.  However,  due  to  time
constraints and limited cooperation from certain stakeholders, the review could not delve as deeply into
specific aspects of the procurement processes as initially intended.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(7)

1.6 Methodology
1.6.1 Approach
The methodology for this Procurement Post-Review is structured into three key phases:

1.  Preliminary Planning and Preparation: Finalizing the work plan, scope, and program for
the  post-procurement  review;  identifying  and  obtaining  essential  contract  information  and
engaging with responsible staff.

2.  Fieldwork: Conducting a thorough review of documents at the offices of the Procuring Entities

(PEs) and physical inspection of implementation sites.

3.  Reporting: Compiling findings, preparing the draft report, presenting it to stakeholders, and

finalizing the report based on feedback.

Phase 1: Preliminary Planning and Preparation

Prior to fieldwork, a detailed work plan was developed, outlining the scope, activities, and timeline for
the  review.  Initial  consultations  were  conducted  with  BPPA,  DAE  officials,  Project  Directors,  and
Procuring Entities to discuss the methodology, scope, and legal obligations.

A comprehensive contract list from five selected Procuring Entities for the financial year 2023-2024
was  gathered.  From  this,  30  contracts  (06  per  PE)  were  randomly  selected,  ensuring  diverse
procurement methods and varying contract values.

With these preparations in place, the Consultant proceeded with the review as per the finalized work
plan.

Phase 2: Fieldwork

The fieldwork phase involved a comprehensive review of selected procurement contracts and physical
site inspections. A documentary review was conducted at PD offices, analyzing procurement contracts
using specified formats for goods, works, and services. Data collection was carried out using BPPA-
defined formats, including Part-1 (General) of the individual contract review format.

A compliance assessment was performed to evaluate contract adherence to relevant Acts, Rules, and
guidelines, including e-GP and development partner regulations, following the contract review format
outlined in Annex-02 of the ToR. Physical inspections were undertaken at DAE project sites where
contract  implementation  had  occurred,  using  the  Annex-02  formats  to  assess  execution  quality  and
adherence to specifications.

An  indicator-based  analysis  was  conducted  based  on  categories  outlined  in  Annex-04  of  the  ToR,
ensuring a thorough evaluation of procurement efficiency. The collected data underwent processing
and validation through compilation, analysis, and cross-referencing to ensure accuracy and consistency.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(8)

Phase 3: Reporting

Following the completion of  fieldwork, the  findings were  compiled  into a  Draft  Final  Report.  This
report  was  then  presented  to  stakeholders  in  a  workshop  facilitated  by  the  Client,  allowing  for
discussions and feedback. Based on stakeholder input, necessary revisions were made to incorporate
observations, suggestions, and recommendations, leading to the submission of the Final Report.

1.6.2 Deliverables
As per the ToR, the consultant is obliged to produce and submit the following reports in the prescribed
manner and within the time frame as instructed by the client, BPPA.

1.7 Limitations
Every  review  task  comes  with  inherent  limitations,  shaped  by  the  nature  of  the  assignment.
Procurement post-reviews are no exception and are particularly prone to certain challenges. During
this review, several limitations were identified, including:
Time Constraints: The review had to be conducted within a limited timeframe, which restricted
the scope and depth of analysis.
Selective Scope: Only specific projects were selected for review, limiting the comprehensiveness
of the procurement post-review process.
Contract Nature: The Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE) primarily engages in Goods
and Works contracts, with minimal involvement in Service contracts. This imbalance may have
restricted insights into the broader procurement dynamics.
Misaligned Perceptions: There is a noticeable tendency to equate audit activities with procurement
post-review, which led to misunderstandings about the objectives of the review. This occasionally
resulted in insufficient cooperation and a lack of mutual respect from stakeholders.
Dependence on Procuring Entity (PE): The reviewer relied heavily on the procuring entity to
supply  the  necessary  information,  as  access  to  the  e-GP  portal  was  not  permitted.  Delays  in
receiving data, fragmented delivery of information across phases, and instances where critical data
were not provided added significant challenges.

These  limitations  collectively  contributed  to  delays  and  impeded  the  review's  efficiency  and
effectiveness.  Addressing  these  challenges  in  future  reviews  would  significantly  enhance  the
quality and timeliness of procurement post-reviews.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(14)

Chapter 2

Project Description and Selection of Contract

2.1 Project Description and Selection of Procurement Contracts

2.1.1 Selections of the Projects
In consultation with the BPPA and concerned officials of DAE, the following projects have been
selected for  procurement  post-review  under  five  different  procuring  entities  for conducting  post
procurement review:

1.  Agricultural  Development  Project  of  Sylhet  Region  through  Modern  Technology

(ADSRMT)

2. Extension of Improved Variety and Technology of Spices Project (EIVTSP)
3. Smallholder Agricultural Competitiveness Project (SACP-RAINS)
4. Agrometeorological Information Systems Development Project (AMISDP)
5.  Upgradation  of  Existing  Plant  Quarantine  Laboratory  at  Central  Packing  House  to

International Standard Project (1st Revised)

2.1.2 Selection of Procurement Contracts
The BPPA tasked the consultant with selecting a sample of 30 completed contracts from the total
contracts fully executed during FY 2023-2024 across five projects (as described earlier), with six
contracts chosen per Procuring Entity (PE). Following an analysis of the Annual Procurement Plans
(APP) for these five projects, the consultant, in collaboration with the BPPA authority, identified
30 contracts for the procurement post-review.

The  selection  process  prioritized  the  following  criteria  to  ensure  a  representative  and  insightful
review:

(a) High value, Medium value, Low value of the contract
(b Method of procurement
(c) Goods, Works and Service, etc.
(d) NCT and ICT Tendering
(e) Manual and e-GP tendering
(f) Location of implementation of the Contract

Upgradation of Existing Plant Quarantine Laboratory at Central
Packing House to International Standard Project (1st Revised)
Dr. Shamim Ahmed
224345600
The primary objective of this project is to transform the existing
physical infrastructure of the Central Packing House into a state-of-
the-art, internationally recognized laboratory for pest identification
within plant quarantine operations. This upgrade aims to align with
international standards, enhancing the laboratory's capacity to
effectively manage plant health risks associated with agricultural
exports. By establishing a robust quarantine laboratory, the project
will ensure compliance with global phytosanitary requirements,
reduce the risk of pest-related trade barriers, and facilitate smoother
export processes.

Additionally, the project seeks to contribute to the economic
empowerment of local farmers by accelerating the export of
agricultural produce. By improving the speed and efficiency of export
certification, farmers will gain quicker access to international
markets, promoting fair pricing and improving their livelihoods.
Ultimately, the project aims to boost Bangladesh's agricultural trade
by enhancing product quality, building trust with international trade
partners, and creating new opportunities for growth in the agricultural
sector.

Chapter 3

Data Analysis and Findings

3.1 Background of BPPA, DAE and Project Management Processes

3.1.1 Background of BPPA
The  Bangladesh  Public  Procurement  Authority  (BPPA),  operating  under  the  Implementation
Monitoring and Evaluation Division (IMED) of the Ministry of Planning, is the principal regulatory
body  responsible  for  public  procurement  governance  in  Bangladesh.  Previously  known  as  the
Central Procurement Technical Unit (CPTU), BPPA was reconstituted under the Bangladesh Public
Procurement Authority Act, 2023 (Act No. 32 of 2023), to strengthen institutional capacity, ensure
transparency,  and  promote  accountability  across  all  public  sector  procurement  activities.  This
transition  marked  a  significant  step  in  the  government's  ongoing  commitment  to  reform  the
procurement landscape to align with international best practices and to maximise value for public
expenditure.

BPPA is mandated to formulate procurement regulations, policies, and guidelines; provide capacity
development support to procuring entities; operate the national e-Government Procurement (e-GP)
system; and monitor compliance across public sector procurement. It operates under a Governing
Board chaired by the Honourable Minister of Planning, with the Chief Executive Officer (CEO)
leading  the  day-to-day management  of its  functions. BPPA’s  activities are  integral  to  achieving
objectives of good governance, sustainable development, and efficient public service delivery.

As  part  of  its  institutional  oversight  mechanism,  BPPA  regularly  undertakes  Procurement  Post
Reviews (PPRs) to assess the extent of compliance with the Public Procurement Act 2006 (PPA
2006)  and  Public  Procurement  Rules  2008  (PPR  2008)  of  various  public  organizations.  The
Procurement Post Review process, conducted by independent individual consultants, involves the
systematic  examination  of  completed  procurement  activities  based  on  a  risk-based  sampling
methodology. In accordance with Section 24 of the PPA 2006 and Rules 45 and 46 of the PPR
2008, the reviews aim to identify procedural weaknesses, instances of non-compliance, and areas
for systemic improvement. The review process evaluates tendering documentation, contract award
decisions,  contract  management  practices,  and  performance  outcomes.  Findings  and
recommendations  from  these  reviews  are  intended  to  support  institutional  capacity  building,
promote  corrective  actions,  and  contribute  to  evidence-based  reforms  for  enhancing  efficiency,
fairness, and transparency in public procurement.

Further details on BPPA’s roles, responsibilities, and operational frameworks can be accessed at
https://cptu.gov.bd.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(24)

3.1.2 Background of DAE

The Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE) was established in 1960. Following Bangladesh's
independence,  the  government  prioritized  strengthening  agricultural  extension  programs  by
establishing the Cotton Development Board, Tobacco Development Board, and Horticulture Board.
In 1975, two new directorates—the Directorate of Agriculture (Extension and Management) and
the Directorate of Jute—were formed to enhance agricultural development. By 1982, six agencies
responsible  for  technology  transfer  services,  including  DAE  (Extension  and  Management),
Directorate of Agriculture (Jute Production), Directorate of Plant Protection, Horticulture Board,
Tobacco  Development  Board,  and  the  Central  Extension  Resources  and  Development  Institute
(CERDI), were consolidated to create the current Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE).

From 1977 to 1990, DAE implemented agricultural extension programs under the "Training and
Inspection" (T&V) approach. However, since 1990, these programs have transitioned to a "group
approach," fostering more collaborative and community-focused agricultural development. In 1996,
the  government  introduced  the  New  Agriculture  Extension  Policy  (NAEP)  to  establish  a  well-
structured  and  strategic  extension  service  framework.  DAE’s  mission  is  to  deliver  efficient,
decentralized,  location-specific,  and  integrated  extension  services  to  all  farmers.  By  improving
access  to  better  knowledge  and  sustainable  practices,  DAE  aims  to  enhance  crop  production,
support farmers’ profitability, and contribute to the country’s socio-economic development.

3.1.3 Mission and Objectives of DAE

The mission of the Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE) is to deliver efficient, effective,
decentralized, and location-specific extension services tailored to the diverse needs of farmers. By
facilitating  access  to  advanced  agricultural  knowledge  and  practices,  DAE  aims  to  enhance
sustainable and profitable crop production. This mission ultimately supports the broader goal of
driving socio-economic development across the country.

3.1.4 Main Wings of DAE and role of Director General

The Ministry of Agriculture oversees the progress of all projects and programs implemented by the
Department  of  Agricultural  Extension  (DAE).  Prior  to  these  reviews,  all  Project  Directors  are
required to submit progress reports detailing achievements against planned targets and allocated
funds under the Annual Development Program (ADP). The Director General, as the head of the
organization, is responsible for overseeing all operational wings of the agency. Additionally, the
Director General chairs the monthly progress review meetings to ensure effective monitoring and
alignment of project activities with organizational objectives.

DAE  conducts  agricultural  extension  and  technology  transfer,  and  financial  and  administrative
activities through eight main wings under it. The eight wings are:

➢  Administration And Finance Wing
➢  Field Services Wing
➢  Crops Wing
➢  Horticulture Wing
➢  Training Wing

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(25)

➢  Plant Protection Wing
➢  Plant Quarantine Wing
➢  Planning, Project Implementation and ICT Wing

For the effective and efficient management of the Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE),
the Director General plays a central coordination role across all eight wings of the organization.
Each wing is tasked with specialized responsibilities to ensure transparency and foster excellence
in  field  extension  activities.  For  example,  the  Planning,  Project  Implementation,  and  ICT  Wing
oversees the planning and implementation of projects and programs under the development budget.
Additionally,  it  handles  key  functions  such  as  procurement,  financial  management,  and
environmental and social compliance.

The Directors of each wing are actively involved in supervising and monitoring both wing-specific
and inter-wing extension activities. Leveraging their expertise and field knowledge, they ensure the
smooth  execution  of  tasks  under  their  respective  domains.  To  enhance  coordination  among  the
wings,  the  Agricultural  Extension  Management  Committee,  chaired  by  the  Director  General,
facilitates overall alignment of departmental activities. During committee meetings, the heads  of
each wing actively participate in discussions, reviews, and decision-making processes, focusing on
field-level developments and addressing challenges within their jurisdictions.

3.1.5 Information Flow in the Department of Agricultural Extension
The  Department  of  Agricultural  Extension  employs  a  bottom-up  approach  to  ensure  a  seamless
flow  of  information  between  the  national  level,  field  staff,  and  farmers.  This  approach  aims  to
facilitate the prompt resolution of farmers' issues while maintaining a decentralized information
management system. Responsibilities are clearly delineated across institutional levels, including
divisions, districts, Upazilas, and blocks.

➢  Farmers  to  Sub-Assistant  Agriculture  Officer:  Sub-Assistant  Agriculture  Officers

(SAAOs) document farmers' problems and the proposed solutions in their diaries.

➢  Sub-Assistant Agriculture Officer to Upazila: Unresolved issues recorded by SAAOs
are  escalated  during  weekly  meetings  or  training  sessions  held  at  the  Upazila  level.
Agricultural Extension Officers (AEOs), supported by Additional Agriculture Officers or
Upazila  Agriculture  Officers,  ensure  the  solutions  are  communicated  promptly  to  the
concerned farmers.

➢  Upazila to District: At the district level monthly meetings, Upazila Agriculture Officers
present unresolved issues. Solutions provided are conveyed back to farmers through the
Deputy Agriculture Officer.

➢  District  to  Regional  Level:  Unresolved  issues  from  district  meetings  are  presented  by
Deputy Directors during regional monthly meetings. The Additional Director of the region
ensures  solutions  are  disseminated  to  farmers.  Any  persistent  issues  are  raised  in  the
Regional  Agricultural  Technical  Coordination  Committee  (RTC)  meetings  for  further
action.

➢  Regional to Headquarters: The Additional Director forwards unresolved matters to the
Director of the Field Services Wing. The DAE Management Committee, relevant national

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(26)

research  institutes,  or  other  specialized  organizations  collaborate  to  address  these
challenges and provide solutions to farmers.

This structured flow ensures efficient problem-solving and fosters effective communication across
all levels of the Department.

3.1.6 DAE Project Management Approach

To ensure the efficient execution of project activities, Project Directors and other key officers are
appointed  on  deputation  from  the  Department  of  Agricultural  Extension  (DAE).  Support  staff,
including computer operators, office assistants, and drivers, are hired on a temporary basis through
outsourcing for the duration of the project. The composition of project personnel is determined by
factors such as the project's type, significance, complexity, and budget.

Field-level activities are implemented by DAE officers operating at the district, Upazila, and block
levels, while project officials oversee the coordination and supervision of all project operations to
ensure alignment with objectives and timelines.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(27)

3.3 Procurement Opportunities
According  to  PPR-2008:  Rule  90(2)(ka),  IFT  advertisements  are  required  to  have  a  wide  daily
circulation. All the reviewed contracts followed the rule for publishing; however, the concept of
wide circulation appears debatable. Some IFTs were published in newspapers such as The Daily
Desh  Rupantor,  Somoyer  Alo,  Banglar  Nabochetona,  Bangla  Navakantha,  The  Daily  Ajker
Probhat, and Bangladesher Alo in Bengali, and The Daily Voice of Asia, Daily Industry, and The
Daily  Post in  English  (Annexure-G).  While this  practice is legally compliant, it  raises  concerns
about whether the spirit of "wide circulation" is consistently upheld.
Procuring Entities (PEs) under the Department of Agricultural Extension (DAE) select newspapers
for  advertisement  based  on  the  list  approved  by  the  Department  of  Films  and  Publications.  In
practice, selections were consistently made from the first 50 newspapers on that list. Consequently,
newspapers with comparatively modest audience reach may have been chosen, potentially limiting
the overall effectiveness of wide dissemination.

(Please see Annexure G for the list of newspaperes where IFTs/REOIs were published)

3.4 Formation of Procurement Committees:
All committee were formed according to the Rule 7(1) & 8(1) of PPR & e-GP guideline: Appendix
2 of BPR and Rule8(1), Schedule II of PPR, & e-GP guideline: Appendix 2 of BPR e-GP guideline
respectively. These committees maintained compliance in terms of membership composition and
operational procedures.

(Please see Annexure H for the full list of procurement committees for 30 contracts of 05 projects)

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(31)

3.5  Official Cost Estimate VS Contract Price

1.  Overall Trend: The majority of contract prices are lower than the official cost estimates
(OCE),  with  only  one  instance  showing  a  positive  variation  (+0.59%  for  P4C4).  This
indicates  effective  procurement  strategies  that  resulted  in  cost  savings  across  most
contracts.

2.  Significant Variations:

➢  The highest negative variation is observed in P3C4 (Goods), where the contract price is
45.67%  lower  than  the  Official  Cost  Estimate  (OCE).  Upon  reviewing  the  tender
documents,  this  significant  deviation  may  be  attributed  to  the  use  of  an  analogous
estimation technique in preparing the OCE, which relied on historical costs from similar—
but not identical—survey assignments. Furthermore, given the highly competitive nature
of  the  market,  the  awarded  firm  submitted  an  aggressively  priced  bid,  capitalizing  on
existing field infrastructure, experienced survey personnel, and digital data collection tools
to minimize operational costs and achieve greater cost-efficiency. This deviation highlights
the need to revisit the cost estimation process, emphasising bottom-up or activity-based
costing  methods,  supplemented  by  current  market  intelligence  and  pre-bid  market
soundings to ensure more accurate forecasting in future procurement exercises.

➢  Similarly,  contracts  P3C3  (Goods)  and  P3C1  (Goods)  also  show  considerable  negative
variations of -37.80% and -36.63%, respectively, suggesting possible discrepancies in cost
estimation or fluctuating market conditions.

3.  Moderate Savings: Many contracts show moderate cost savings between -0.04% to -10%,
reflecting consistent procurement efficiency. For example, P1C1 (-3.21%), P1C2 (-5.10%),
and P2C2 (-17.27%) show varied yet healthy savings.

4.  Minimal Variation: Contract P2C1 demonstrates the least variation with -0.07%, showing
precise cost estimation and procurement alignment. Similarly, P5C6 also shows an almost
negligible difference of -0.04%.

5.  Exception in Data: In the case of P5C5, the official cost estimate is not found,

6.  Strategic Implications: The data reflects strong procurement performance overall, with an
emphasis on budget control. However, the significant negative deviations in some contracts
may necessitate a review of estimation methods to improve accuracy and reduce potential
resource allocation issues in future projects.

(Please see Annexure I for comparison of official cost estimate and contract price for 30
contracts of 05 projects)

Observations in Procurement Process

1.  Overall Compliance: The majority of procurement processes demonstrated compliance
across most procedural stages. However, there were notable inconsistencies, particularly in
the timeline adherence for specific stages.

2.  Non-Compliance Patterns: Non-compliance was predominantly observed in the second
stage of the procurement process (opening to evaluation), with significant delays noted in
multiple instances. For example, P1C2, P1C5, P1C6, P4C1, P5C2, P5C3, and P5C5 showed
non-compliance with extended delays, ranging from 52 to 79 days.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(36)

3.7.1.3 Observations in competition:
1.  Tender Document Sold :

➢  The  “AMISDP"  project  had  the  highest  tender  document  sales  with  49  units,

demonstrating strong market interest.

➢  "ADSRMT" and "EIVTSP" projects also showed robust competition with 37 and

39 units sold, respectively.

➢  The “Central Packing" and "SACP" projects had moderate sales, with 33 and 26

units, indicating relatively lower market engagement.

2.  Submission of Tenders:

➢  "AMISDP"  led  in  submission  rates  with  36  submissions,  suggesting  high

conversion from interest to participation.

➢  “ADSRMT“, “EIVTSP“, and “Central Packing” projects followed with 26, 28 and

25 submissions, respectively.

➢  "SACP" had lowest submissions (14), showing a gap between interest and actual

participation.

3.  Responsive and Non-Responsive Tenders :

➢  The  highest  number  of  responsive  tenders  was  in  “EIVTSP"  (17),  closely  followed  by

“AMISDP" (16).

➢  "ADSRMT," "SACP," and "Central Packing" showed lower responsiveness, with “SACP"

having the fewest responsive tenders (8).

➢  Non-responsiveness was most prominent in "ADSRMT" (17), suggesting a need for better

clarity in requirements or vendor preparedness.

➢  In 01 tender (P2C1), only 01 (single) tender was submitted and deemed responsive.
➢  In 12 tenders (P1C1, P1C3, P1C4, P1C5, P1C6, P4C2, P4C3, P5C1, P5C2, P5C3, P5C5 &
P5C6), only 01 tender became responsive despite submitted tenders were more than 01.
➢  In  03  tenders  P3C4  (QCBS),  P3C5  (SSS)  &  P4C4  (CQS),  only  01  tender  became

responsive due to the method of the tendering process.

➢  In  the  works  tenders  (P1C5  and  P2C5),  two  tenderers  were  rejected  by  the  e-GP  auto-
generated  system  for  noncompliance  with  the  (±)  10%  threshold  of  the  Official  Cost
Estimate (OCE).

4.  Single (01) responsiveness in 16 packages:

In 16 packages, only one tender was deemed responsive despite multiple submissions. In
01  package,  a  single  tender  was  submitted;  and  in  03  packages,  01  tender  became
responsive due to the chosen tendering method. Most notably, in 12 packages, submitted
tenders were rendered non-responsive due to non-compliance with eligibility criteria, lack
of  relevant  experience,
technical
specifications.

insufficient  qualifications,  or  deviations  from

The  high  rate  of  non-responsiveness  across  the  12  packages  due  to  several  interlinked
factors.  In  many  cases,  the  eligibility  and  qualification  criteria  may  have  been  too
restrictive,  disqualifying  bidders  who  lacked  specific  experience  or  financial  capacity.
Additionally,  many  tenderers  failed  to  fully  understand  or  comply  with  technical

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

(37)

specifications, leading to incomplete or non-conforming submissions. Also, the absence of
manufacturer authorizations—contributed to disqualification.

5.  General Trends and Insights:
➢  While “AMISDP" had the highest initial interest (49 sold), the conversion to submissions
and responsiveness was relatively low, highlighting a potential issue in tender requirements
or vendor alignment.

➢  High non-responsiveness in "ADSRMT" (17) and "Central Packing“ (15) suggests a need

to review the evaluation criteria or provide better guidance to potential bidders.

➢  Projects like "SACP" and "AMISDP" showed moderate competition with opportunities to

improve submission and responsiveness rates.

6.  Market Participation:

➢  Certain suppliers, such as M/S R J Trade International (03 contracts) and SMR Biswas
Engineers  Ltd  (02  contracts),  have  consistently  secured  contracts  under  the
Agricultural  Development  in  Sylhet  Region  through  Modern  Technologies  Project.
This pattern indicates a concentrated supplier base.

➢  25 out of 30 awarded contracts were secured by contractors based in Dhaka, indicating
a strong concentration of suppliers in the capital. Only  5 contracts were awarded to
firms  from  outside  Dhaka,  highlighting  low  engagement  from  contractors  in  other
districts.

➢  A high reliance on Dhaka-based contractors may indicate a lack of competition from

regional suppliers or barriers preventing them from participating effectively.

3.7.2.2 Observations in Contract Management (Time Overrun):

Contract Management (Time Overun)

(Figure 4)

Observations in Contract Management (Time overrun):
The review of contract management revealed that 16 contracts were successfully completed within
the original timeframe, demonstrating effective planning and execution in these cases.
A total of six contracts experienced minor delays of less than 20 days, including contracts P1C4,
P1C6,  P3C1,  P3C2,  P3C3,  and  P4C2.  These  delays,  while  relatively  short,  indicate  minor
disruptions stemmed from contractor performance.
08 contracts (P1C2, P1C3, P4C1, P4C4, P5C1, P5C2, P5C3, and P5C6) encountered substantial
delays, due to complications related to the opening of Letters of Credit (L/C).
In 01  contract (P5C7 – delay 34 days), time extension document was not found.
The  Extension  of  Improved  Variety  and  Technology  of  Spices  Project  (EIVTSP)  was  executed
without any time overruns, indicating efficient contract administration and adherence to scheduled
timelines.

3.7.2.3 Observations in Contract Management (Cost Overrun):

Contract Management (Cost Overun)

(Figure 5)

Observations in Contract Management (Cost overrun):
The analysis of contract management in terms of cost overruns reveals that the majority of contracts
(25  out  of  30,  accounting  for  83%)  were  successfully  completed  within  the  original  budget,
demonstrating  effective  cost  control  and  adherence  to  financial  planning.  This  indicates  that
procurement and project management strategies were largely efficient in preventing unnecessary
cost escalations.
However, five  contracts (17%) all from    AMISDP  project (P4C1,  P4C2,  P4C3, P4C5  &  P4C6)
exceeded the original budget, requiring additional financial allocations. This cost overrun was due
to changes in project scope, all variations were done as per PPR-2008, Rule 78, 79 & 80.

78

3.9 Project Management Structure of DAE

The  Planning,  Project  Implementation,  and  ICT  Wing  of  the  Department  of  Agricultural  Extension
(DAE) supports project planning, monitoring, and evaluation.

A  10-13members  Project  Implementation  Committee  (PIC),  led  by  the  Director  General  of  DAE,
manages  project  execution  and  resolves  implementation  issues.  A  12-15  members  Project  Steering
Committee (PSC), chaired by the Secretary of the Ministry of Agriculture, provides strategic and policy
guidance.

Projects are governed by the Development Project Proposal (DPP), adhering to the Public Procurement
Act 2006 and Public Procurement Rules 2008. The procurement process in the Project Management
Unit (PMU) typically follows:

Upazila  Agriculture  Office  →  District  Agriculture  Office  →  Deputy  Project  Director  →  Project
Director → Head of Procuring Entity (HOPE).

The Director General of DAE serves as the Head of Procuring Entity. Procurement procedures may
vary based on specific project requirements.

3.10 Strengths and Weaknesses of PEs under review

3.10.1 Strengths

1.  Adequate Manpower for Procurement Oversight – The Project Management Units (PMUs) are
well-staffed with the necessary personnel to effectively coordinate and monitor procurement
contracts at the central level.

2.  Annual  Procurement  Plan  (APP)  Compliance  –  PEs  successfully  prepared  and  obtained
approval  for  their  APPs  from  the  Head  of  the  Procuring  Entity  (HOPE)  or  the  Authorized
Officer (AO) as per regulatory requirements.

3.  Transparent and Systematic Tendering Process – All tenders were processed through the e-GP
system, with advertisements published in both Bengali and English national newspapers, as
well as on the e-GP portal and DAE website, ensuring wider accessibility and compliance.
4.  Use of Standardized Procurement Documents – PEs adhered to Standard Tender Documents
(STDs) and provided comprehensive details in Tender Data Sheets (TDS) & Proposal Data
Sheet (PDS) and other required formats in line with e-GP guidelines.

5.  Formation  of  Evaluation  Committees  as  per  Guidelines  –  The  Tender  Opening  Committee
(TOC) and Tender Evaluation Committee (TEC) were established in compliance with e-GP
regulations to ensure fair and structured evaluation.

6.  Extensive  supply  chain  network—spanning  Region,  District,  Upazila,  and  Union/Ward
levels—enables  effective  last-mile  service  delivery  and  ensures  broad  outreach  across  rural
communities.

7.  Timely  Issuance  of  Notifications  of  Award  (NOA)  –NOAs  were  issued  within  7  days  of

contract approval, demonstrating efficiency in the contract award process.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

79

8.  Prompt  Contract  Execution  –  Contract  agreements  were  signed  within  28  days  from  the
issuance of the NOA, reflecting efficient contract finalization and within tender validity period.
9.  Absence  of  Complaints  or  Disputes  –  No  formal  complaints  or  disputes  were  recorded,

indicating a well-managed procurement process with minimal procedural challenges.

3.10.2 Weaknesses

1.  Limited  Competition  in  Procurement  –  Several  procurement  processes  faced  low  bidder

participation, potentially limiting competitive pricing and value for money.

2.  Issues in Official Cost Estimates (OCEs) – In some cases, OCEs were not thoroughly assessed,
which may have contributed to minor budgetary misalignments and cost planning challenges.
3.  Gaps  in  Procurement  Documentation  –  Procurement  documentation  was  not  consistently
maintained at a satisfactory level. In certain cases, critical documents such as time extension
approvals were missing, affecting auditability and compliance tracking.

4.  Inadequate Performance Security Coverage – In some contracts, the performance security in
the form of a bank guarantee did not fully cover the contract period, warranty/defect liability
period, plus the required additional 28 days, exposing the projects to financial and performance
risks.

5.  Lack of training in procurement and project management remain a key weakness, hindering
consistent application of best practices and regulatory compliance across DAE operations.

3.12 Assessment of Procurement Management Capacity and Training Needs
In  alignment  with  BPPA  (formerly  CPTU)  guidelines,  the  Procurement  Post  Review  evaluates  the
procurement  management  capacity  of  Procuring  Entities  (PEs)  and  offers  recommendations  for
improvement. The assessment covers key areas, including:
•  Human Resource Development (HRD) Facilities

•  Procurement Training Coverage

Discussions with DAE’s Training and Development Wing indicate that approximately 3,000 Class I
officers  are  currently  employed.  As  of  June  2024,  514  officers  have  completed  the  3-week  Public
Procurement Rules (PPR) training conducted by BPPA, with the figure expected to reach 650.
Although  no  comprehensive  statistics  are  available  on  e-GP  training,  current  trends  suggest  that  a
higher number of officials receive e-GP training compared to PPR, reflecting a growing emphasis on
digital procurement proficiency.
Despite  the  need  for  specialized  training,  DAE  lacks  a  dedicated  training  institute  with  adequate
facilities.  While  the  Training  and  Development  Wing  organizes  short-term  training  sessions  and
seminars, these remain insufficient to meet the diverse capacity-building requirements of its workforce.
However, the reviewed projects demonstrate a positive trend, with nearly all incorporating in-country
and overseas training to enhance procurement competency.
The National Agricultural Training Academy (NATA), under the Ministry of Agriculture, serves as a
key training center for agricultural professionals and offers specialized public procurement training,
complementing DAE’s capacity-building efforts.
Given DAE’s extensive procurement activities across operational and development budgets and with
development partners, there is a pressing need for a well-trained workforce to ensure efficient project
implementation and robust procurement processes.
As an immediate measure, DAE could collaborate with NATA, BPPA, and the Ministry of Agriculture
to conduct 3-week PPR training sessions, expanding training coverage, strengthening project execution,
and improving procurement efficiency.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension

82

3.13 Formation of Procurement Wing in DAE
While  establishing  a  procurement  cell  was  outside  the  scope  of  the  procurement  post-review,  its
progress  was  monitored  due  to  its  importance  for  enhancing  DAE's  procurement  efficiency  and
capacity. A functional procurement cell can streamline practices and bolster officer capabilities.

In a meeting on March 8, 2018, chaired by the former Director General of CPTU, NSPSOs, including
DAE, were urged to create procurement cells with 5-10 trained staff from existing resources. Following
this, CPTU issued a directive on March 18, 2018, to implement the decision.

DAE subsequently formed an 8-member procurement cell under the DIMAPPP project. CPTU also
provided 212 laptops on January 7, 2021, to improve procurement operations. However, the review
found  that  the  cell  lacked  dedicated  office  space,  furniture,  and  logistical  support,  leaving  it  non-
functional. The laptops were distributed across various offices, with none allocated to the procurement
cell.

On November 17, 2020, BPPA issued guidelines outlining the roles and responsibilities of procurement
cells. Despite these measures, an operational procurement cell at DAE remains absent.

DAE’s workforce includes many officers trained in public procurement, e-GP, and holding advanced
qualifications like MCIPS and MPSM, highlighting the potential for a robust procurement cell.

Given DAE’s extensive procurement activities, management should establish a functional cell of 8-10
trained officers, initially assigning procurement duties alongside their regular roles. The cell should
operate as per TOR guidelines, with BPPA’s support as needed.

Procurement Post Review of the procurement conducted in the FY 2023-24 by the Department of Agricultrual Extension
